	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Johann Sebastian Bach</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Bach" redirects here. For Bach's grandson, see <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach_(painter)" title="Johann Sebastian Bach (painter)">Johann Sebastian Bach (painter)</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Bach_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Bach (disambiguation)">Bach (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">German composer</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<table class="infobox biography vcard" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold"><div class="fn" style="display:inline">Johann Sebastian Bach</div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Johann_Sebastian_Bach.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Johann Sebastian Bach.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Johann_Sebastian_Bach.jpg/220px-Johann_Sebastian_Bach.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="271" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Johann_Sebastian_Bach.jpg/330px-Johann_Sebastian_Bach.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Johann_Sebastian_Bach.jpg/440px-Johann_Sebastian_Bach.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="591" /></a><div>Portrait of Bach, aged 61, <span class="nowrap">by <a href="/wiki/Elias_Gottlob_Haussmann" title="Elias Gottlob Haussmann">E. G. Haussmann</a>, 1748</span></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Born</th><td>21 March 1685 <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">(O.S.)</a><br />31 March 1685 <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">(N.S.)</a><br /><div style="display:inline" class="birthplace"><a href="/wiki/Eisenach" title="Eisenach">Eisenach</a></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Died</th><td>28 July 1750<span style="display:none">(1750-07-28)</span> (aged&#160;65)<br /><div style="display:inline" class="deathplace"><a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">Leipzig</a></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><div style="white-space:nowrap;">Works</div></th><td><a href="/wiki/List_of_compositions_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="List of compositions by Johann Sebastian Bach">List of compositions</a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center">Signature</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Johann_Sebastian_Bach_signature.svg" class="image"><img alt="Johann Sebastian Bach signature.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Johann_Sebastian_Bach_signature.svg/150px-Johann_Sebastian_Bach_signature.svg.png" decoding="async" width="150" height="30" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Johann_Sebastian_Bach_signature.svg/225px-Johann_Sebastian_Bach_signature.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Johann_Sebastian_Bach_signature.svg/300px-Johann_Sebastian_Bach_signature.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="72" /></a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Johann Sebastian Bach</b><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> (31 March&#160;&#91;<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> 21 March&#93;&#160;1685&#160;&#8211;&#32;28 July 1750) was a German composer and musician of the <a href="/wiki/Baroque_music" title="Baroque music">Baroque period</a>. He is known for instrumental compositions such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Art_of_Fugue" class="mw-redirect" title="Art of Fugue">Art of Fugue</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Brandenburg_Concertos" title="Brandenburg Concertos">Brandenburg Concertos</a></i>, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Goldberg_Variations" title="Goldberg Variations">Goldberg Variations</a></i>, and for vocal music such as the <i><a href="/wiki/St_Matthew_Passion" title="St Matthew Passion">St&#160;Matthew Passion</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_B_minor" title="Mass in B minor">Mass in B&#160;minor</a>. Since the 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Bach_Revival" class="mw-redirect" title="Bach Revival">Bach Revival</a> he has been generally regarded as one of the greatest composers of the <a href="/wiki/Western_art_music" class="mw-redirect" title="Western art music">Western art musical</a> canon.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bach_family" title="Bach family">Bach family</a> already counted several composers when Johann Sebastian was born as the last child of a city musician in <a href="/wiki/Eisenach" title="Eisenach">Eisenach</a>. After being orphaned at age 10, he lived for five years with his eldest brother <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christoph_Bach_(organist_at_Ohrdruf)" title="Johann Christoph Bach (organist at Ohrdruf)">Johann Christoph Bach</a>, after which he continued his musical development in <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCneburg" title="Lüneburg">Lüneburg</a>. From 1703 he was back in <a href="/wiki/Thuringia" title="Thuringia">Thuringia</a>, working as a musician for <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> churches in <a href="/wiki/Arnstadt" title="Arnstadt">Arnstadt</a> and <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BChlhausen" title="Mühlhausen">Mühlhausen</a> and, for longer stretches of time, at courts in <a href="/wiki/Saxe-Weimar" title="Saxe-Weimar">Weimar</a>, where he expanded his <a href="/wiki/Pipe_organ" title="Pipe organ">organ</a> repertory, and <a href="/wiki/Anhalt-K%C3%B6then" title="Anhalt-Köthen">Köthen</a>, where he was mostly engaged with <a href="/wiki/Chamber_music" title="Chamber music">chamber music</a>. From 1723 he was employed as <a href="/wiki/Thomaskantor" title="Thomaskantor">Thomaskantor</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cantor_(Christianity)" title="Cantor (Christianity)">cantor</a> at <a href="/wiki/St._Thomas_School,_Leipzig" title="St. Thomas School, Leipzig">St.&#160;Thomas</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">Leipzig</a>. He composed music for the principal <a href="/wiki/Lutheran" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a> churches of the city, and for its university's student ensemble <a href="/wiki/Collegium_Musicum" title="Collegium Musicum">Collegium Musicum</a>. From 1726 <a href="/wiki/List_of_compositions_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach_printed_during_his_lifetime" title="List of compositions by Johann Sebastian Bach printed during his lifetime">he published</a> some of his <a href="/wiki/Keyboard_instrument" title="Keyboard instrument">keyboard</a> music. In Leipzig, as had happened during some of his earlier positions, he had difficult relations with his employer, a situation that was little remedied when he was granted the title of court composer by his sovereign, <a href="/wiki/Augustus_III_of_Poland" title="Augustus III of Poland">Augustus</a>, Elector of Saxony and King of Poland, in 1736. In the last decades of his life he reworked and extended many of his earlier compositions. He died of complications after eye surgery in 1750 at the age of 65.
</p><p>Bach enriched established German styles through his mastery of <a href="/wiki/Counterpoint" title="Counterpoint">counterpoint</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harmony" title="Harmony">harmonic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Motif_(music)" title="Motif (music)">motivic</a> organisation, and his adaptation of rhythms, forms, and textures from abroad, particularly from Italy and France. <a href="/wiki/List_of_compositions_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="List of compositions by Johann Sebastian Bach">Bach's compositions</a> include <a href="/wiki/Bach_cantata" title="Bach cantata">hundreds of cantatas</a>, both <a href="/wiki/Church_cantata_(Bach)" title="Church cantata (Bach)">sacred</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_secular_cantatas_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="List of secular cantatas by Johann Sebastian Bach">secular</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wolff5_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff5-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> He <a href="/wiki/Bach%27s_church_music_in_Latin" title="Bach&#39;s church music in Latin">composed Latin church music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Passions_(Bach)" title="Passions (Bach)">Passions</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_masses,_passions_and_oratorios_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach#Oratorios_and_associated_cantatas" title="List of masses, passions and oratorios by Johann Sebastian Bach">oratorios</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_motets_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="List of motets by Johann Sebastian Bach">motets</a>. He often adopted <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_hymn" title="Lutheran hymn">Lutheran hymns</a>, not only in his larger vocal works, but for instance also in <a href="/wiki/List_of_chorale_harmonisations_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="List of chorale harmonisations by Johann Sebastian Bach">his four-part chorales</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_songs_and_arias_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="List of songs and arias by Johann Sebastian Bach">sacred songs</a>. He wrote extensively <a href="/wiki/List_of_organ_compositions_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="List of organ compositions by Johann Sebastian Bach">for organ</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_keyboard_and_lute_compositions_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="List of keyboard and lute compositions by Johann Sebastian Bach">for other keyboard instruments</a>. He <a href="/wiki/List_of_concertos_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="List of concertos by Johann Sebastian Bach">composed concertos</a>, for instance <a href="/wiki/List_of_orchestral_works_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach#Violin_concertos_(BWV_1041–1043)" title="List of orchestral works by Johann Sebastian Bach">for violin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Keyboard_concertos_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Keyboard concertos by Johann Sebastian Bach">for harpsichord</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Suite_(Bach)" title="Suite (Bach)">suites</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_chamber_music_works_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="List of chamber music works by Johann Sebastian Bach">as chamber music</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Orchestral_suites_(Bach)" title="Orchestral suites (Bach)">for orchestra</a>. <a href="/wiki/List_of_fugal_works_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="List of fugal works by Johann Sebastian Bach">Many of his works employ</a> contrapuntal genres such as <a href="/wiki/Fugue" title="Fugue">fugue</a>.
</p><p>Throughout the 18th century Bach was mostly renowned as an <a href="/wiki/Organist" title="Organist">organist</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> while his keyboard music, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Well-Tempered_Clavier" title="The Well-Tempered Clavier">The Well-Tempered Clavier</a></i>, was appreciated for its didactic qualities.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The 19th century saw the publication of some major <a href="/wiki/Bach_biographies" class="mw-redirect" title="Bach biographies">Bach biographies</a>, and by the end of that century all of his known music had been printed. Dissemination of scholarship on the composer continued through periodicals exclusively devoted to him, and publications such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Bach-Werke-Verzeichnis" title="Bach-Werke-Verzeichnis">Bach-Werke-Verzeichnis</a></i> (BWV, a numbered catalogue of his works) and new critical editions of his compositions. His music was further popularised through a multitude <a href="/wiki/List_of_transcriptions_of_compositions_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="List of transcriptions of compositions by Johann Sebastian Bach">of arrangements</a>, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Air_on_the_G_String" title="Air on the G String">Air on the G String</a></i>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Musical_style"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Musical style</span></a>
<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JSBWohnorte.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/JSBWohnorte.svg/240px-JSBWohnorte.svg.png" decoding="async" width="240" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/JSBWohnorte.svg/360px-JSBWohnorte.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/JSBWohnorte.svg/480px-JSBWohnorte.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="329" data-file-height="331" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JSBWohnorte.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Places where Bach lived</div></div></div>
<p>Bach was born in 1685 in <a href="/wiki/Eisenach" title="Eisenach">Eisenach</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Saxe-Eisenach" title="Saxe-Eisenach">duchy of Saxe-Eisenach</a>, into an extensive musical family. His father, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Ambrosius_Bach" title="Johann Ambrosius Bach">Johann Ambrosius Bach</a>, was the director of the town musicians, and all of his uncles were professional musicians. His father probably taught him to play the <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harpsichord" title="Harpsichord">harpsichord</a>, and his brother <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christoph_Bach_(organist_at_Ohrdruf)" title="Johann Christoph Bach (organist at Ohrdruf)">Johann Christoph Bach</a> taught him the <a href="/wiki/Clavichord" title="Clavichord">clavichord</a> and exposed him to much of the contemporary music.<sup id="cite_ref-Wolff19,46_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff19,46-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Apparently on his own initiative, Bach attended <a href="/wiki/St._Michaelis,_L%C3%BCneburg" title="St. Michaelis, Lüneburg">St. Michael's School</a> in <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCneburg" title="Lüneburg">Lüneburg</a> for two years. After graduating, he held several musical posts across Germany, including <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Kapellmeister" title="Kapellmeister">Kapellmeister</a></i> (director of music) to <a href="/wiki/Leopold,_Prince_of_Anhalt-K%C3%B6then" title="Leopold, Prince of Anhalt-Köthen">Leopold, Prince of Anhalt-Köthen</a>, and <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Thomaskantor" title="Thomaskantor">Thomaskantor</a></i> in Leipzig, a position of music director at the main Lutheran churches and educator at the <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Thomasschule_zu_Leipzig" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomasschule zu Leipzig">Thomasschule</a></i>. He received the title of "Royal Court Composer" from <a href="/wiki/Augustus_III_of_Poland" title="Augustus III of Poland">Augustus&#160;III</a> in 1736.<sup id="cite_ref-baroquemusic_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baroquemusic-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Miles_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miles-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Bach's health and vision declined in 1749, and he died on 28 July 1750.
</p>
<h3><span id="Childhood_.281685.E2.80.931703.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_(1685–1703)">Childhood (1685–1703)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Johann_Ambrosius_Bach.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Johann_Ambrosius_Bach.jpg/240px-Johann_Ambrosius_Bach.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="341" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e0/Johann_Ambrosius_Bach.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="426" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Johann_Ambrosius_Bach.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Johann Ambrosius Bach, Bach's father</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenacher_Gesangbuch_Tugendlieder.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Eisenacher_Gesangbuch_Tugendlieder.jpg/240px-Eisenacher_Gesangbuch_Tugendlieder.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="410" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Eisenacher_Gesangbuch_Tugendlieder.jpg/360px-Eisenacher_Gesangbuch_Tugendlieder.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Eisenacher_Gesangbuch_Tugendlieder.jpg/480px-Eisenacher_Gesangbuch_Tugendlieder.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1152" data-file-height="1970" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenacher_Gesangbuch_Tugendlieder.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Page from the <i>Neues vollständiges Eisenachisches Gesangbuch</i>, the Lutheran hymnal that was in use in the Eisenach of Bach's youth<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lvnaeburgvm_um_1682.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Lvnaeburgvm_um_1682.jpg/240px-Lvnaeburgvm_um_1682.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Lvnaeburgvm_um_1682.jpg/360px-Lvnaeburgvm_um_1682.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Lvnaeburgvm_um_1682.jpg/480px-Lvnaeburgvm_um_1682.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3865" data-file-height="3088" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lvnaeburgvm_um_1682.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lüneburg, some two decades before Bach's stay in that town: St Michael's pictured in lower right</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bach_family" title="Bach family">Bach family</a></div>
<p>Johann Sebastian Bach was born in <a href="/wiki/Eisenach" title="Eisenach">Eisenach</a>, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Saxe-Eisenach" title="Saxe-Eisenach">duchy of Saxe-Eisenach</a>, in present-day Germany, on 21 March 1685 <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> (31 March 1685 <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">N.S.</a>). He was the son of <a href="/wiki/Johann_Ambrosius_Bach" title="Johann Ambrosius Bach">Johann Ambrosius Bach</a>, the director of the town musicians, and <a href="/wiki/Maria_Elisabeth_L%C3%A4mmerhirt" title="Maria Elisabeth Lämmerhirt">Maria Elisabeth Lämmerhirt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> He was the eighth and youngest child of Johann Ambrosius,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> who likely taught him violin and basic music theory.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> His uncles were all professional musicians, whose posts included church organists, court chamber musicians, and composers. One uncle, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christoph_Bach_(musician_at_Arnstadt)" title="Johann Christoph Bach (musician at Arnstadt)">Johann Christoph Bach</a> (1645–1693), introduced him to the organ, and an older second cousin, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Ludwig_Bach" title="Johann Ludwig Bach">Johann Ludwig Bach</a> (1677–1731), was a well-known composer and violinist.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bach's mother died in 1694, and his father died eight months later.<sup id="cite_ref-Miles_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miles-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The 10-year-old Bach moved in with his eldest brother, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christoph_Bach_(organist_at_Ohrdruf)" title="Johann Christoph Bach (organist at Ohrdruf)">Johann Christoph Bach</a> (1671–1721), the organist at <a href="/wiki/Michaeliskirche_(Ohrdruf)" title="Michaeliskirche (Ohrdruf)">St.&#160;Michael's Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ohrdruf" title="Ohrdruf">Ohrdruf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saxe-Gotha-Altenburg" title="Saxe-Gotha-Altenburg">Saxe-Gotha-Altenburg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> There he studied, performed, and copied music, including his own brother's, despite being forbidden to do so because scores were so valuable and private, and blank ledger paper of that type was costly.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> He received valuable teaching from his brother, who instructed him on the <a href="/wiki/Clavichord" title="Clavichord">clavichord</a>. J.&#160;C.&#160;Bach exposed him to the works of great composers of the day, including South German composers such as <a href="/wiki/Johann_Pachelbel" title="Johann Pachelbel">Johann Pachelbel</a> (under whom Johann Christoph had studied) and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Jakob_Froberger" title="Johann Jakob Froberger">Johann Jakob Froberger</a>; North German composers;<sup id="cite_ref-Wolff19,46_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff19,46-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Frenchmen, such as <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lully" title="Jean-Baptiste Lully">Jean-Baptiste Lully</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Marchand" title="Louis Marchand">Louis Marchand</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marin_Marais" title="Marin Marais">Marin Marais</a>; and the Italian clavierist <a href="/wiki/Girolamo_Frescobaldi" title="Girolamo Frescobaldi">Girolamo Frescobaldi</a>. Also during this time, he was taught theology, Latin, Greek, French, and Italian at the local <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">gymnasium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Baroquenet_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baroquenet-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 3 April 1700, Bach and his schoolfriend Georg Erdmann—who was two years Bach's elder—were enrolled in the prestigious St. Michael's School in <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCneburg" title="Lüneburg">Lüneburg</a>, some two weeks' travel north of Ohrdruf.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EidamChI_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EidamChI-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Their journey was probably undertaken mostly on foot.<sup id="cite_ref-Baroquenet_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baroquenet-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EidamChI_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EidamChI-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> His two years there were critical in exposing Bach to a wider range of European culture. In addition to singing in the choir, he played the school's three-manual organ and harpsichords.<sup id="cite_ref-Baroquenet_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baroquenet-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> He came into contact with sons of aristocrats from northern Germany who were sent to the highly selective school to prepare for careers in other disciplines.
</p><p>While in Lüneburg, Bach had access to <a href="/wiki/St._John%27s_Church,_L%C3%BCneburg" title="St. John&#39;s Church, Lüneburg">St.&#160;John's Church</a> and possibly used the church's famous organ from 1553, since it was played by his organ teacher <a href="/wiki/Georg_B%C3%B6hm" title="Georg Böhm">Georg Böhm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stauffer_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stauffer-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Because of his musical talent, Bach had significant contact with Böhm while a student in Lüneburg, and he also took trips to nearby <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> where he observed "the great North German organist <a href="/wiki/Johann_Adam_Reincken" title="Johann Adam Reincken">Johann Adam Reincken</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Stauffer_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stauffer-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Stauffer reports the discovery in 2005 of the <a href="/wiki/Organ_tablature" title="Organ tablature">organ tablatures</a> that Bach wrote, while still in his teens, of works by Reincken and <a href="/wiki/Dieterich_Buxtehude" title="Dieterich Buxtehude">Dieterich Buxtehude</a>, showing "a disciplined, methodical, well-trained teenager deeply committed to learning his craft".<sup id="cite_ref-Stauffer_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stauffer-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Weimar.2C_Arnstadt.2C_and_M.C3.BChlhausen_.281703.E2.80.931708.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Weimar,_Arnstadt,_and_Mühlhausen_(1703–1708)">Weimar, Arnstadt, and Mühlhausen (1703–1708)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Orgeln.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Orgeln.jpg/240px-Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Orgeln.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Orgeln.jpg/360px-Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Orgeln.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Orgeln.jpg/480px-Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Orgeln.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1824" data-file-height="1368" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arnstadt_Bachkirche_Orgeln.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Wender" title="Johann Friedrich Wender">Wender</a> organ Bach played in Arnstadt</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Young_Bach2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Young_Bach2.jpg/240px-Young_Bach2.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="328" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/Young_Bach2.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="342" data-file-height="468" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Young_Bach2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of the young Bach (disputed)<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In January 1703, shortly after graduating from St. Michael's and being turned down for the post of organist at <a href="/wiki/Sangerhausen" title="Sangerhausen">Sangerhausen</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Bach was appointed court musician in the chapel of Duke <a href="/wiki/Johann_Ernst_III,_Duke_of_Saxe-Weimar" title="Johann Ernst III, Duke of Saxe-Weimar">Johann Ernst&#160;III</a> in <a href="/wiki/Weimar" title="Weimar">Weimar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> His role there is unclear, but it probably included menial, non-musical duties. During his seven-month tenure at Weimar, his reputation as a keyboardist spread so much that he was invited to inspect the new organ and give the inaugural recital at the New Church (now <a href="/wiki/Bach_Church,_Arnstadt" title="Bach Church, Arnstadt">Bach Church</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Arnstadt" title="Arnstadt">Arnstadt</a>, located about 30 kilometres (19&#160;mi) southwest of Weimar.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In August 1703, he became the organist at the New Church, with light duties, a relatively generous salary, and a new organ tuned in a temperament that allowed music written in a wider range of keys to be played.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Despite strong family connections and a musically enthusiastic employer, tension built up between Bach and the authorities after several years in the post. Bach was dissatisfied with the standard of singers in the choir. He called one of them a "Zippel Fagottist" (<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/weenie" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:weenie">weenie</a> bassoon player). Late one evening this student, named Geyersbach, went after Bach with a stick. Bach filed a complaint against Geyersbach with the authorities. They acquitted Geyersbach with a minor reprimand and ordered Bach to be more moderate regarding the musical qualities he expected from his students. Some months later Bach upset his employer by a prolonged absence from Arnstadt: after obtaining leave for four weeks, he was absent for around four months in 1705–1706 to visit the organist and composer <a href="/wiki/Dieterich_Buxtehude" title="Dieterich Buxtehude">Dieterich Buxtehude</a> in the northern city of <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCbeck" title="Lübeck">Lübeck</a>. The visit to Buxtehude involved a 450-kilometre (280&#160;mi) journey each way, reportedly on foot.<sup id="cite_ref-Wolff2000pp83ff_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff2000pp83ff-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1706, Bach applied for a post as organist at the <a href="/wiki/Divi_Blasii" title="Divi Blasii">Blasius Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BChlhausen" title="Mühlhausen">Mühlhausen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WolffP102_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WolffP102-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> As part of his application, he had a <a href="/wiki/Cantata" title="Cantata">cantata</a> performed on Easter, 24 April 1707, likely an early version of his <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Christ_lag_in_Todes_Banden,_BWV_4" title="Christ lag in Todes Banden, BWV 4">Christ lag in Todes Banden</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> A month later Bach's application was accepted and he took up the post in July.<sup id="cite_ref-WolffP102_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WolffP102-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The position included significantly higher remuneration, improved conditions, and a better choir. Four months after arriving at Mühlhausen, Bach married <a href="/wiki/Maria_Barbara_Bach" title="Maria Barbara Bach">Maria Barbara Bach</a>, his second cousin. Bach was able to convince the church and town government at Mühlhausen to fund an expensive renovation of the organ at the Blasius Church. In 1708 Bach wrote <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Gott_ist_mein_K%C3%B6nig,_BWV_71" title="Gott ist mein König, BWV 71">Gott ist mein König</a></i>, a festive <a href="/wiki/Church_cantata_(Bach)#New_council" title="Church cantata (Bach)">cantata for the inauguration of the new council</a>, which was published at the council's expense.<sup id="cite_ref-Baroquenet_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baroquenet-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Return_to_Weimar_.281708.E2.80.931717.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_Weimar_(1708–1717)">Return to Weimar (1708–1717)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BWV1001-cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/BWV1001-cropped.jpg/240px-BWV1001-cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="380" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/BWV1001-cropped.jpg/360px-BWV1001-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/BWV1001-cropped.jpg/480px-BWV1001-cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="535" data-file-height="847" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BWV1001-cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bach's autograph of the first movement of the <a href="/wiki/Sonatas_and_Partitas_for_Solo_Violin_(Bach)#Sonata_No._1_in_G_minor,_BWV_1001" title="Sonatas and Partitas for Solo Violin (Bach)">Sonata No.&#160;1 in G&#160;minor for solo violin (BWV&#160;1001)</a> – <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sonata_in_in_G_m_J.S.Bach_(1_mov_prelude).ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ee/Sonata_in_in_G_m_J.S.Bach_%281_mov_prelude%29.ogg" class="internal" title="Sonata in in G m J.S.Bach (1 mov prelude).ogg">Audio</a></span></span></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Erschallet,_ihr_Lieder,_erklinget,_ihr_Saiten!_BWV_172#Background" title="Erschallet, ihr Lieder, erklinget, ihr Saiten! BWV 172">Erschallet, ihr Lieder, erklinget, ihr Saiten! BWV 172 §&#160;Background</a></div>
<p>Bach left Mühlhausen in 1708, returning to Weimar this time as organist and from 1714 <i lang="de" title="German language text">Konzertmeister</i> (director of music) at the ducal court, where he had an opportunity to work with a large, well-funded contingent of professional musicians.<sup id="cite_ref-Baroquenet_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baroquenet-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Bach and his wife moved into a house close to the ducal palace.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Later the same year, their first child, Catharina Dorothea, was born, and Maria Barbara's elder, unmarried sister joined them. She remained to help run the household until her death in 1729. Three sons were also born in Weimar: <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Friedemann_Bach" title="Wilhelm Friedemann Bach">Wilhelm Friedemann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Philipp_Emanuel_Bach" title="Carl Philipp Emanuel Bach">Carl Philipp Emanuel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_Bernhard_Bach" title="Johann Gottfried Bernhard Bach">Johann Gottfried Bernhard</a>. Johann Sebastian and Maria Barbara had three more children, who however did not live to their first birthday, including twins born in 1713.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bach's time in Weimar was the start of a sustained period of composing keyboard and orchestral works. He attained the proficiency and confidence to extend the prevailing structures and include influences from abroad. He learned to write dramatic openings and employ the dynamic rhythms and harmonic schemes found in the music of Italians such as <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Vivaldi" title="Antonio Vivaldi">Vivaldi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arcangelo_Corelli" title="Arcangelo Corelli">Corelli</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Torelli" title="Giuseppe Torelli">Torelli</a>. Bach absorbed these stylistic aspects in part by transcribing Vivaldi's string and wind <a href="/wiki/Concerto" title="Concerto">concertos</a> for harpsichord and organ; many of these transcribed works are still regularly performed. Bach was particularly attracted to the Italian style, in which one or more solo instruments alternate section-by-section with the full orchestra throughout a <a href="/wiki/Movement_(music)" title="Movement (music)">movement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Weimar, Bach continued to play and compose for the organ and perform concert music with the duke's ensemble.<sup id="cite_ref-Baroquenet_20-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baroquenet-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> He also began to write the <a href="/wiki/Prelude_(music)" title="Prelude (music)">preludes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fugue" title="Fugue">fugues</a> which were later assembled into his monumental work <i><a href="/wiki/The_Well-Tempered_Clavier" title="The Well-Tempered Clavier">The Well-Tempered Clavier</a></i> ("clavier" meaning clavichord or harpsichord),<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> consisting of two books,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> each containing 24 preludes and fugues in every <a href="/wiki/Major_scale" title="Major scale">major</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minor_scale" title="Minor scale">minor</a> key. Bach also started work on the <i><a href="/wiki/Orgelb%C3%BCchlein" title="Orgelbüchlein">Little Organ Book</a></i> in Weimar, containing traditional <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_chorale" title="Lutheran chorale">Lutheran chorale</a> tunes set in complex textures. In 1713, Bach was offered a post in <a href="/wiki/Halle_(Saale)" title="Halle (Saale)">Halle</a> when he advised the authorities during a renovation by Christoph Cuntzius of the main organ in the west gallery of the <a href="/wiki/Marktkirche_Unser_Lieben_Frauen" title="Marktkirche Unser Lieben Frauen">Market Church of Our Dear Lady</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sadie1998p205_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sadie1998p205-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the spring of 1714, Bach was promoted to <i lang="de" title="German language text">Konzertmeister</i>, an honour that entailed performing a church cantata monthly in the castle church.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The first three cantatas in the new series Bach composed in Weimar were <span lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Himmelsk%C3%B6nig,_sei_willkommen,_BWV_182" title="Himmelskönig, sei willkommen, BWV 182"><i>Himmelskönig, sei willkommen</i>, BWV 182</a></span>, for <a href="/wiki/Palm_Sunday" title="Palm Sunday">Palm Sunday</a>, which coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Annunciation" title="Annunciation">Annunciation</a> that year; <span lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Weinen,_Klagen,_Sorgen,_Zagen,_BWV_12" title="Weinen, Klagen, Sorgen, Zagen, BWV 12"><i>Weinen, Klagen, Sorgen, Zagen</i>, BWV 12</a></span>, for <a href="/wiki/Jubilate_Sunday" title="Jubilate Sunday">Jubilate Sunday</a>; and <span lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Erschallet,_ihr_Lieder,_erklinget,_ihr_Saiten!_BWV_172" title="Erschallet, ihr Lieder, erklinget, ihr Saiten! BWV 172"><i>Erschallet, ihr Lieder, erklinget, ihr Saiten!</i>&#160;&#160;BWV 172</a></span> for <a href="/wiki/Pentecost" title="Pentecost">Pentecost</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wolff30_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff30-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Bach's first Christmas cantata, <span lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Christen,_%C3%A4tzet_diesen_Tag,_BWV_63" title="Christen, ätzet diesen Tag, BWV 63"><i>Christen, ätzet diesen Tag</i>, BWV 63</a></span>, was premiered in 1714 or 1715.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leipzig_Universit%C3%A4tskirche.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Leipzig_Universit%C3%A4tskirche.JPG/240px-Leipzig_Universit%C3%A4tskirche.JPG" decoding="async" width="240" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Leipzig_Universit%C3%A4tskirche.JPG/360px-Leipzig_Universit%C3%A4tskirche.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Leipzig_Universit%C3%A4tskirche.JPG/480px-Leipzig_Universit%C3%A4tskirche.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2904" data-file-height="2166" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leipzig_Universit%C3%A4tskirche.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <span lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Paulinerkirche,_Leipzig" title="Paulinerkirche, Leipzig">Paulinerkirche</a></span> in Leipzig: in 1717 Bach had tested the new organ in this church.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1717, Bach eventually fell out of favour in Weimar and, according to a translation of the court secretary's report, was jailed for almost a month before being unfavourably dismissed: "On November&#160;6, [1717], the quondam concertmaster and organist Bach was confined to the County Judge's place of detention for too stubbornly forcing the issue of his dismissal and finally on December&#160;2 was freed from arrest with notice of his unfavourable discharge."<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="K.C3.B6then_.281717.E2.80.931723.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Köthen_(1717–1723)">Köthen (1717–1723)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bach_Seal.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Bach_Seal.svg/240px-Bach_Seal.svg.png" decoding="async" width="240" height="108" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Bach_Seal.svg/360px-Bach_Seal.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Bach_Seal.svg/480px-Bach_Seal.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1338" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bach_Seal.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bach's seal (centre), used throughout his Leipzig years. It contains the superimposed letters <i><span class="nowrap">J S B</span></i> in mirror image topped with a crown. The flanking letters  illustrate the arrangement on the seal.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:St._Thomas_Church,_Leipzig.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/St._Thomas_Church%2C_Leipzig.jpg/240px-St._Thomas_Church%2C_Leipzig.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="321" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/St._Thomas_Church%2C_Leipzig.jpg/360px-St._Thomas_Church%2C_Leipzig.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/St._Thomas_Church%2C_Leipzig.jpg/480px-St._Thomas_Church%2C_Leipzig.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1952" data-file-height="2608" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:St._Thomas_Church,_Leipzig.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/St._Thomas_Church,_Leipzig" title="St. Thomas Church, Leipzig">St. Thomas Church, Leipzig</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Leopold,_Prince_of_Anhalt-K%C3%B6then" title="Leopold, Prince of Anhalt-Köthen">Leopold, Prince of Anhalt-Köthen</a>, hired Bach to serve as his <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Kapellmeister" title="Kapellmeister">Kapellmeister</a></i> (director of music) in 1717. Prince Leopold, himself a musician, appreciated Bach's talents, paid him well and gave him considerable latitude in composing and performing. The prince was a <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinist</a> and did not use elaborate music in his worship; accordingly, most of Bach's work from this period was secular,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> including the <a href="/wiki/Orchestral_suites_(Bach)" title="Orchestral suites (Bach)">orchestral suites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cello_Suites_(Bach)" title="Cello Suites (Bach)">cello suites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sonatas_and_Partitas_for_Solo_Violin_(Bach)" title="Sonatas and Partitas for Solo Violin (Bach)">sonatas and partitas for solo violin</a>, and <i>Brandenburg Concertos</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Bach also composed secular cantatas for the court, such as <span lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Die_Zeit,_die_Tag_und_Jahre_macht,_BWV_134a" title="Die Zeit, die Tag und Jahre macht, BWV 134a"><i>Die Zeit, die Tag und Jahre macht</i>, BWV 134a</a></span>. A significant influence upon Bach's musical development during his years with the prince is recorded by Stauffer as Bach's "complete embrace of dance music, perhaps the most important influence on his mature style other than his adoption of Vivaldi's music in Weimar."<sup id="cite_ref-Stauffer_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stauffer-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite being born in the same year and only about 130 kilometres (80&#160;mi) apart, Bach and <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">Handel</a> never met. In 1719, Bach made the 35-kilometre (22&#160;mi) journey from <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6then_(Anhalt)" title="Köthen (Anhalt)">Köthen</a> to <a href="/wiki/Halle_(Saale)" title="Halle (Saale)">Halle</a> with the intention of meeting Handel; however, Handel had left the town.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> In 1730, Bach's oldest son, Wilhelm Friedemann, travelled to Halle to invite Handel to visit the Bach family in Leipzig, but the visit did not take place.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 7 July 1720, while Bach was away in <a href="/wiki/Karlovy_Vary" title="Karlovy Vary">Carlsbad</a> with Prince Leopold, Bach's wife suddenly died.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The following year, he met <a href="/wiki/Anna_Magdalena_Bach" title="Anna Magdalena Bach">Anna Magdalena Wilcke</a>, a young, highly gifted soprano 16 years his junior, who performed at the court in Köthen; they married on 3 December 1721.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Together they had 13 more children, 6 of whom survived into adulthood: <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Heinrich_Bach" title="Gottfried Heinrich Bach">Gottfried Heinrich</a>; Elisabeth Juliane Friederica (1726–1781); <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christoph_Friedrich_Bach" title="Johann Christoph Friedrich Bach">Johann Christoph Friedrich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christian_Bach" title="Johann Christian Bach">Johann Christian</a>, who both, especially Johann Christian, became significant musicians; Johanna Carolina (1737–1781); and Regina Susanna (1742–1809).<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Leipzig_.281723.E2.80.931750.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Leipzig_(1723–1750)">Leipzig (1723–1750)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leipzig_Nikolaikirche_um_1850.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Leipzig_Nikolaikirche_um_1850.jpg/240px-Leipzig_Nikolaikirche_um_1850.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Leipzig_Nikolaikirche_um_1850.jpg/360px-Leipzig_Nikolaikirche_um_1850.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Leipzig_Nikolaikirche_um_1850.jpg/480px-Leipzig_Nikolaikirche_um_1850.jpg 2x" data-file-width="577" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leipzig_Nikolaikirche_um_1850.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/St._Nicholas_Church,_Leipzig" title="St. Nicholas Church, Leipzig">St. Nicholas Church, Leipzig</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1850</span></div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg/240px-Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="487" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg/360px-Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg/480px-Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="709" data-file-height="1440" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zimmermannsches_Caffeehaus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Caf%C3%A9_Zimmermann" title="Café Zimmermann">Café Zimmermann</a>, Leipzig, where the Collegium Musicum performed</div></div></div>
<p>In 1723, Bach was appointed <i><a href="/wiki/Thomaskantor" title="Thomaskantor">Thomaskantor</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Cantor_(Christianity)" title="Cantor (Christianity)">Cantor</a> of the <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Thomasschule_zu_Leipzig" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomasschule zu Leipzig">Thomasschule</a></i> at the <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/St._Thomas_Church,_Leipzig" title="St. Thomas Church, Leipzig">Thomaskirche</a></i> (St. Thomas Church) in Leipzig, which provided music for four churches in the city: the <i lang="de" title="German language text">Thomaskirche</i> and <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/St._Nicholas_Church,_Leipzig" title="St. Nicholas Church, Leipzig">Nikolaikirche</a></i> (St. Nicholas Church) and to a lesser extent the <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/St._Matthew,_Leipzig" title="St. Matthew, Leipzig">Neue Kirche</a></i> (New Church) and <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/St._Peter,_Leipzig" title="St. Peter, Leipzig">Peterskirche</a></i> (St. Peter's Church).<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> This was "the leading cantorate in Protestant Germany",<sup id="cite_ref-Wolff253_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff253-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> located in the mercantile city in the <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Saxony" title="Electorate of Saxony">Electorate of Saxony</a>, which he held for 27 years until his death. During that time he gained further prestige through honorary appointments at the courts of Köthen and Weissenfels, as well as that of the Elector <a href="/wiki/Augustus_II_the_Strong" title="Augustus II the Strong">Frederick Augustus</a> (who was also <a href="/wiki/King_of_Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Poland">King of Poland</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Dresden" title="Dresden">Dresden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wolff253_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff253-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Bach frequently disagreed with his employer, Leipzig's city council, which he regarded as "penny-pinching".<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Appointment_in_Leipzig">Appointment in Leipzig</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Johann_Kuhnau" title="Johann Kuhnau">Johann Kuhnau</a> had been Thomaskantor in Leipzig from 1701 until his death on 5 June 1722. Bach had visited Leipzig during Kuhnau's tenure: in 1714 he attended the service at the St.&#160;Thomas Church on the first Sunday of Advent,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> and in 1717 he had tested the organ of the <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Paulinerkirche,_Leipzig" title="Paulinerkirche, Leipzig">Paulinerkirche</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> In 1716 Bach and Kuhnau had met on the occasion of the testing and inauguration of an organ in Halle.<sup id="cite_ref-Sadie1998p205_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sadie1998p205-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After being offered the position, Bach was invited to Leipzig only after <a href="/wiki/Georg_Philipp_Telemann" title="Georg Philipp Telemann">Georg Philipp Telemann</a> indicated that he would not be interested in relocating to Leipzig.<sup id="cite_ref-BL_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BL-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Telemann went to Hamburg, where he "had his own struggles with the city's senate".<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bach was required to instruct the students of the <i lang="de" title="German language text">Thomasschule</i> in singing and provide church music for the main churches in Leipzig. He was also assigned to teach Latin but was allowed to employ four "prefects" (deputies) to do this instead. The prefects also aided with musical instruction.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> A cantata was required for the church services on Sundays and additional church holidays during the <a href="/wiki/Church_cantata_(Bach)" title="Church cantata (Bach)">liturgical year</a>.
</p>
<h4><span id="Cantata_cycle_years_.281723.E2.80.931729.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cantata_cycle_years_(1723–1729)">Cantata cycle years (1723–1729)</span></h4>
<p>Bach usually led performances of his <a href="/wiki/Bach_cantata" title="Bach cantata">cantatas</a>, most of which were composed within three years of his relocation to Leipzig. The first was <span lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Die_Elenden_sollen_essen,_BWV_75" title="Die Elenden sollen essen, BWV 75"><i>Die Elenden sollen essen</i>, BWV 75</a></span>, performed in the <i lang="de" title="German language text">Nikolaikirche</i> on 30 May 1723, the first Sunday after <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Sunday" title="Trinity Sunday">Trinity</a>. Bach collected his cantatas in annual cycles. Five are mentioned in obituaries, three are extant.<sup id="cite_ref-Wolff30_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff30-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Of the more than 300 cantatas which Bach composed in Leipzig, over 100 have been lost to posterity.<sup id="cite_ref-Wolff5_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff5-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Most of these works expound on the Gospel readings prescribed for every Sunday and feast day in the Lutheran year. Bach started a second annual cycle the first Sunday after Trinity of 1724 and composed only <a href="/wiki/Chorale_cantata_(Bach)" title="Chorale cantata (Bach)">chorale cantatas</a>, each based on a single church hymn. These include <span lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/O_Ewigkeit,_du_Donnerwort,_BWV_20" title="O Ewigkeit, du Donnerwort, BWV 20"><i>O&#160;Ewigkeit, du Donnerwort</i>, BWV 20</a></span>, <span lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Wachet_auf,_ruft_uns_die_Stimme,_BWV_140" title="Wachet auf, ruft uns die Stimme, BWV 140"><i>Wachet auf, ruft uns die Stimme</i>, BWV 140</a></span>, <span lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Nun_komm,_der_Heiden_Heiland,_BWV_62" title="Nun komm, der Heiden Heiland, BWV 62"><i>Nun komm, der Heiden Heiland</i>, BWV 62</a></span>, and <span lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Wie_sch%C3%B6n_leuchtet_der_Morgenstern,_BWV_1" title="Wie schön leuchtet der Morgenstern, BWV 1"><i>Wie schön leuchtet der Morgenstern</i>, BWV 1</a></span>.
</p><p>Bach drew the soprano and alto choristers from the school and the tenors and basses from the school and elsewhere in Leipzig. Performing at weddings and funerals provided extra income for these groups; it was probably for this purpose, and for in-school training, that he wrote at least six <a href="/wiki/Motet" title="Motet">motets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> As part of his regular church work, he performed other composers' motets, which served as formal models for his own.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bach's predecessor as cantor, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Kuhnau" title="Johann Kuhnau">Johann Kuhnau</a>, had also been music director for the <i lang="de" title="German language text">Paulinerkirche</i>, the church of <a href="/wiki/Leipzig_University" title="Leipzig University">Leipzig University</a>. But when Bach was installed as cantor in 1723, he was put in charge only of music for festal (church holiday) services at the <i lang="de" title="German language text">Paulinerkirche</i>; his petition to also provide music for regular Sunday services there (for a corresponding salary increase) went all the way to the Elector but was denied. After this, in 1725, Bach "lost interest" in working even for festal services at the <i lang="de" title="German language text">Paulinerkirche</i> and appeared there only on "special occasions".<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The <i lang="de" title="German language text">Paulinerkirche</i> had a much better and newer (1716) organ than did the <i lang="de" title="German language text">Thomaskirche</i> or the <i lang="de" title="German language text">Nikolaikirche</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Bach was not required to play any organ in his official duties, but it is believed he liked to play on the <i lang="de" title="German language text">Paulinerkirche</i> organ "for his own pleasure".<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bach broadened his composing and performing beyond the liturgy by taking over, in March 1729, the directorship of the <a href="/wiki/Collegium_Musicum" title="Collegium Musicum">Collegium Musicum</a>, a secular performance ensemble started by Telemann. This was one of the dozens of private societies in the major German-speaking cities that were established by musically active university students; these societies had become increasingly important in public musical life and were typically led by the most prominent professionals in a city. In the words of <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Wolff" title="Christoph Wolff">Christoph Wolff</a>, assuming the directorship was a shrewd move that "consolidated Bach's firm grip on Leipzig's principal musical institutions".<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Year round, Leipzig's <i>Collegium Musicum</i> performed regularly in venues such as the <a href="/wiki/Caf%C3%A9_Zimmermann" title="Café Zimmermann">Café Zimmermann</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Coffeehouse" title="Coffeehouse">coffeehouse</a> on Catherine Street off the main market square. Many of Bach's works during the 1730s and 1740s were written for and performed by the <i>Collegium Musicum</i>; among these were parts of his <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Clavier-%C3%9Cbung" title="Clavier-Übung">Clavier-Übung</a></i> (<i>Keyboard Practice</i>) and many of his violin and <a href="/wiki/Keyboard_concertos_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Keyboard concertos by Johann Sebastian Bach">keyboard concertos</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Baroquenet_20-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baroquenet-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Middle_years_of_the_Leipzig_period_.281730.E2.80.931739.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_years_of_the_Leipzig_period_(1730–1739)">Middle years of the Leipzig period (1730–1739)</span></h4>
<p>In 1733, Bach composed a <a href="/wiki/Missa_in_B_minor,_BWV_232_I" title="Missa in B minor, BWV 232 I">Missa</a> (Kyrie and Gloria) which he later incorporated in his Mass in B minor. He presented the manuscript to the Elector in an eventually successful bid to persuade the prince to give him the title of Court Composer.<sup id="cite_ref-baroquemusic_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baroquemusic-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> He later extended this work into a full mass by adding a <span lang="la" title="Latin language text">Credo</span>, <span lang="la" title="Latin language text">Sanctus</span>, and <span lang="la" title="Latin language text">Agnus Dei</span>, the music for which was partly based on his own cantatas and partly original. Bach's appointment as Court Composer was an element of his long-term struggle to achieve greater bargaining power with the Leipzig council. Between 1737 and 1739, Bach's former pupil <a href="/wiki/Carl_Gotthelf_Gerlach" title="Carl Gotthelf Gerlach">Carl Gotthelf Gerlach</a> held the directorship of the <i>Collegium Musicum</i>.
</p><p>In 1735 Bach started to prepare his first publication of organ music, which was printed as the <a href="/wiki/Clavier-%C3%9Cbung_III" title="Clavier-Übung III">third <i>Clavier-Übung</i></a> in 1739.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> From around that year he started to compile and compose the set of preludes and fugues for harpsichord that would become his second book of <i>The Well-Tempered Clavier</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Final_years_and_death_.281740.E2.80.931750.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years_and_death_(1740–1750)">Final years and death (1740–1750)</span></h4>
<p>From 1740 to 1748 Bach copied, transcribed, expanded or programmed music in an older <a href="/wiki/Polyphony" title="Polyphony">polyphonic</a> style (<i><a href="/wiki/Prima_pratica" title="Prima pratica">stile antico</a></i>) by, among others, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pierluigi_da_Palestrina" title="Giovanni Pierluigi da Palestrina">Palestrina</a> (<a href="/wiki/BNB_I/P/2" class="mw-redirect" title="BNB I/P/2">BNB&#160;I/P/2</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johann_Caspar_Kerll" title="Johann Caspar Kerll">Kerll</a> (<a href="/wiki/BWV_241" class="mw-redirect" title="BWV 241">BWV&#160;241</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Torri" title="Pietro Torri">Torri</a> (<a href="/wiki/BWV_Anh._30" class="mw-redirect" title="BWV Anh. 30">BWV&#160;Anh.&#160;30</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Bassani" title="Giovanni Battista Bassani">Bassani</a> (<a href="/wiki/BWV_1081" class="mw-redirect" title="BWV 1081">BWV&#160;1081</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Gasparini" title="Francesco Gasparini">Gasparini</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Missa_Canonica_(Gasparini)" class="mw-redirect" title="Missa Canonica (Gasparini)">Missa Canonica</a></i>)<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Caldara" title="Antonio Caldara">Caldara</a> (<a href="/wiki/BWV_1082" class="mw-redirect" title="BWV 1082">BWV&#160;1082</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Bach's own style shifted in the last decade of his life, showing an increased integration of polyphonic structures and canons and other elements of the <i>stile antico</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-LISA_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LISA-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> His fourth and last <i>Clavier-Übung</i> volume, the <i><a href="/wiki/Goldberg_Variations" title="Goldberg Variations">Goldberg Variations</a></i>, for two-manual harpsichord, contained nine canons and was published in 1741.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Throughout this period, Bach also continued to adopt music of contemporaries such as <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">Handel</a> (<a href="/wiki/BNB_I/K/2" class="mw-redirect" title="BNB I/K/2">BNB&#160;I/K/2</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Heinrich_St%C3%B6lzel" title="Gottfried Heinrich Stölzel">Stölzel</a> (<a href="/wiki/BWV_200" class="mw-redirect" title="BWV 200">BWV&#160;200</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> and gave many of his own earlier compositions, such as the <i>St Matthew</i> and <i>St John</i> Passions and the <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Eighteen_Chorale_Preludes" title="Great Eighteen Chorale Preludes">Great Eighteen Chorale Preludes</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> their final revisions. He also programmed and adapted music by composers of a younger generation, including <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Pergolesi" title="Giovanni Battista Pergolesi">Pergolesi</a> (<a href="/wiki/BWV_1083" class="mw-redirect" title="BWV 1083">BWV&#160;1083</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> and his own students such as <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottlieb_Goldberg" title="Johann Gottlieb Goldberg">Goldberg</a> (<a href="/wiki/BNB_I/G/2" class="mw-redirect" title="BNB I/G/2">BNB&#160;I/G/2</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1746 Bach was preparing to enter <a href="/wiki/Lorenz_Christoph_Mizler" title="Lorenz Christoph Mizler">Lorenz Christoph Mizler</a>'s <a href="/w/index.php?title=Correspondierende_Societ%C3%A4t_der_musicalischen_Wissenschaften&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Correspondierende Societät der musicalischen Wissenschaften (page does not exist)">Society of Musical Sciences</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Correspondierende_Societ%C3%A4t_der_musicalischen_Wissenschaften" class="extiw" title="de:Correspondierende Societät der musicalischen Wissenschaften">de</a>&#93;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In order to be admitted Bach had to submit a composition, for which he chose his <a href="/wiki/Canonic_Variations_on_%22Vom_Himmel_hoch_da_komm%27_ich_her%22" title="Canonic Variations on &quot;Vom Himmel hoch da komm&#39; ich her&quot;">Canonic Variations on "Vom Himmel hoch da komm' ich her"</a>, and a portrait, which was painted by <a href="/wiki/Elias_Gottlob_Haussmann" title="Elias Gottlob Haussmann">Elias Gottlob Haussmann</a> and featured Bach's <i><a href="/wiki/BWV_1076" class="mw-redirect" title="BWV 1076">Canon triplex á 6 Voc</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> In May 1747, Bach visited the court of King <a href="/wiki/Frederick_the_Great" title="Frederick the Great">Frederick&#160;II of Prussia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Potsdam" title="Potsdam">Potsdam</a>. The king played a theme for Bach and challenged him to improvise a fugue based on his theme. Bach obliged, playing a three-part fugue on one of Frederick's <a href="/wiki/Fortepiano" title="Fortepiano">fortepianos</a>, which was a new type of instrument at the time. Upon his return to Leipzig he composed a set of fugues and canons, and a trio sonata, based on the <i>Thema Regium</i> (theme of the king). Within a few weeks this music was published as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Musical_Offering" title="The Musical Offering">The Musical Offering</a></i> and dedicated to Frederick. The <i><a href="/wiki/Sch%C3%BCbler_Chorales" title="Schübler Chorales">Schübler Chorales</a></i>, a set of six chorale preludes transcribed from cantata movements Bach had composed some two decades earlier, were published within a year.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Around the same time, the set of five canonic variations which Bach had submitted when entering Mizler's society in 1747 were also printed.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Two large-scale compositions occupied a central place in Bach's last years. From around 1742 he wrote and revised the various canons and fugues of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Art_of_Fugue" title="The Art of Fugue">The Art of Fugue</a></i>, which he continued to prepare for publication until shortly before his death.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> After extracting a cantata, <a href="/wiki/BWV_191" class="mw-redirect" title="BWV 191">BWV&#160;191</a>, from his <a href="/wiki/Mass_for_the_Dresden_court_(Bach)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mass for the Dresden court (Bach)">1733 Kyrie-Gloria Mass for the Dresden court</a> in the mid 1740s, Bach expanded that <a href="/wiki/Mass_(music)" title="Mass (music)">setting</a> into his <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_B_minor" title="Mass in B minor">Mass in B minor</a> in the last years of his life. Stauffer describes it as "Bach's most universal church work. Consisting mainly of recycled movements from cantatas written over a thirty-five-year period, it allowed Bach to survey his vocal pieces one last time and pick select movements for further revision and refinement."<sup id="cite_ref-Stauffer_23-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stauffer-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Although the complete mass was never performed during the composer's lifetime, it is considered to be among the greatest choral works in history.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 1749, Bach's daughter Elisabeth Juliane Friederica married his pupil <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christoph_Altnickol" title="Johann Christoph Altnickol">Johann Christoph Altnickol</a>. Bach's health was, however, declining. On 2 June, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_von_Br%C3%BChl" title="Heinrich von Brühl">Heinrich von Brühl</a> wrote to one of the Leipzig <a href="/wiki/Burgomaster" title="Burgomaster">burgomasters</a> to request that his music director, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottlob_Harrer" title="Johann Gottlob Harrer">Johann Gottlob Harrer</a>, fill the <i lang="de" title="German language text">Thomaskantor</i> and <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Director musices</i> posts "upon the eventual&#160;... decease of Mr. Bach".<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Becoming blind, Bach underwent eye surgery, in March 1750 and again in April, by the British eye surgeon <a href="/wiki/John_Taylor_(oculist)" title="John Taylor (oculist)">John Taylor</a>, a man widely understood today as a charlatan and believed to have blinded hundreds of people.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Bach died on 28 July 1750 from complications due to the unsuccessful treatment.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> An inventory drawn up a few months after Bach's death shows that his estate included five <a href="/wiki/Harpsichord" title="Harpsichord">harpsichords</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Lautenwerck" title="Lautenwerck">lute-harpsichords</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violins</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">violas</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Cello" title="Cello">cellos</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Viol" title="Viol">viola da gamba</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Lute" title="Lute">lute</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Spinet" title="Spinet">spinet</a>, along with 52 "sacred books", including works by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> and <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The composer's son Carl Philipp Emanuel saw to it that <i>The Art of Fugue</i>, although still unfinished, was published in 1751.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Together with one of the composer's former students, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Agricola" title="Johann Friedrich Agricola">Johann Friedrich Agricola</a>, the son also wrote the obituary ("<a href="/wiki/Bach%27s_Nekrolog" title="Bach&#39;s Nekrolog">Nekrolog</a>"), which was published in Mizler's <a href="/w/index.php?title=Musikalische_Bibliothek&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Musikalische Bibliothek (page does not exist)"><i>Musikalische Bibliothek</i></a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Musikalische_Bibliothek" class="extiw" title="de:Musikalische Bibliothek">de</a>&#93;</span>, the organ of the Society of Musical Sciences, in 1754.<sup id="cite_ref-Nekrolog_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nekrolog-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_style">Musical style</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bach_Calov-Bibel_2_Chr_5,13.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Bach_Calov-Bibel_2_Chr_5%2C13.jpg/300px-Bach_Calov-Bibel_2_Chr_5%2C13.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="374" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Bach_Calov-Bibel_2_Chr_5%2C13.jpg/450px-Bach_Calov-Bibel_2_Chr_5%2C13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/47/Bach_Calov-Bibel_2_Chr_5%2C13.jpg 2x" data-file-width="481" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bach_Calov-Bibel_2_Chr_5,13.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A handwritten note by Bach in his copy of the <a href="/wiki/Calov_Bible" title="Calov Bible">Calov Bible</a>. The note next to <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/2_Chronicles#5:13" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/2 Chronicles">2&#160;Chronicles&#32;5&#58;13</a> reads: "NB Bey einer andächtigen Musiq ist allezeit Gott mit seiner Gnaden Gegenwart" (<a href="/wiki/Nota_bene" title="Nota bene"><span class="nowrap">N(ota) B(ene)</span></a> In a music of worship God is always present with his grace).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg/300px-Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg/450px-Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg/600px-Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1770" data-file-height="1080" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bach_Matth%C3%A4uspassion_O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a href="/wiki/O_Haupt_voll_Blut_und_Wunden" class="mw-redirect" title="O Haupt voll Blut und Wunden">O Haupt voll Blut und Wunden</a>": the four-part chorale setting as included in the <i>St. Matthew Passion</i></div></div></div>
