<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mizler1754p158-BachNekrologFirstPage.pdf" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Mizler1754p158-BachNekrologFirstPage.pdf/page1-240px-Mizler1754p158-BachNekrologFirstPage.pdf.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="395" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Mizler1754p158-BachNekrologFirstPage.pdf/page1-360px-Mizler1754p158-BachNekrologFirstPage.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Mizler1754p158-BachNekrologFirstPage.pdf/page1-480px-Mizler1754p158-BachNekrologFirstPage.pdf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1275" data-file-height="2100" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mizler1754p158-BachNekrologFirstPage.pdf" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First page of <a href="/wiki/Bach%27s_Nekrolog" title="Bach&#39;s Nekrolog">Bach's Nekrolog</a> by Carl Philipp Emanuel Bach and Johann Friedrich Agricola, as published in Mizler's <i>Musikalische Bibliothek</i>, Volume IV Part 1 (1754). Despite the errors contained in it, this obituary of less than 20 pages is arguably "the richest and most trustworthy" source on Bach produced before the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Altes_Bachdenkmal_(Leipzig)_-_Holzstich.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Altes_Bachdenkmal_%28Leipzig%29_-_Holzstich.jpg/240px-Altes_Bachdenkmal_%28Leipzig%29_-_Holzstich.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="336" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Altes_Bachdenkmal_%28Leipzig%29_-_Holzstich.jpg/360px-Altes_Bachdenkmal_%28Leipzig%29_-_Holzstich.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Altes_Bachdenkmal_%28Leipzig%29_-_Holzstich.jpg/480px-Altes_Bachdenkmal_%28Leipzig%29_-_Holzstich.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1801" data-file-height="2525" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Altes_Bachdenkmal_(Leipzig)_-_Holzstich.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Image of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=19th_century_Bach_memorial_(Leipzig)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="19th century Bach memorial (Leipzig) (page does not exist)">Bach memorial</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Altes_Bach-Denkmal_in_Leipzig" class="extiw" title="de:Altes Bach-Denkmal in Leipzig">de</a>&#93;</span> erected by Felix Mendelssohn in Leipzig in 1843</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_J.S._Bach_in_Leipzig.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Statue_of_J.S._Bach_in_Leipzig.jpg/240px-Statue_of_J.S._Bach_in_Leipzig.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="321" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Statue_of_J.S._Bach_in_Leipzig.jpg/360px-Statue_of_J.S._Bach_in_Leipzig.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Statue_of_J.S._Bach_in_Leipzig.jpg/480px-Statue_of_J.S._Bach_in_Leipzig.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1952" data-file-height="2608" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_J.S._Bach_in_Leipzig.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1908 Statue of Bach in front of the Thomaskirche in Leipzig</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arnstadt_Bachkirche_au%C3%9Fen_Chor_03.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Arnstadt_Bachkirche_au%C3%9Fen_Chor_03.jpg/240px-Arnstadt_Bachkirche_au%C3%9Fen_Chor_03.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Arnstadt_Bachkirche_au%C3%9Fen_Chor_03.jpg/360px-Arnstadt_Bachkirche_au%C3%9Fen_Chor_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Arnstadt_Bachkirche_au%C3%9Fen_Chor_03.jpg/480px-Arnstadt_Bachkirche_au%C3%9Fen_Chor_03.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2588" data-file-height="2769" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arnstadt_Bachkirche_au%C3%9Fen_Chor_03.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The church in Arnstadt where Bach had been the organist from 1703 to 1707. In 1935 the church was renamed to "Bachkirche".</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fotothek_df_roe-neg_0002806_004_Besucher_der_Messe_in_der_Thomaskirche_zu_Ehren_Bachs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Fotothek_df_roe-neg_0002806_004_Besucher_der_Messe_in_der_Thomaskirche_zu_Ehren_Bachs.jpg/240px-Fotothek_df_roe-neg_0002806_004_Besucher_der_Messe_in_der_Thomaskirche_zu_Ehren_Bachs.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="377" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Fotothek_df_roe-neg_0002806_004_Besucher_der_Messe_in_der_Thomaskirche_zu_Ehren_Bachs.jpg/360px-Fotothek_df_roe-neg_0002806_004_Besucher_der_Messe_in_der_Thomaskirche_zu_Ehren_Bachs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Fotothek_df_roe-neg_0002806_004_Besucher_der_Messe_in_der_Thomaskirche_zu_Ehren_Bachs.jpg/480px-Fotothek_df_roe-neg_0002806_004_Besucher_der_Messe_in_der_Thomaskirche_zu_Ehren_Bachs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="522" data-file-height="820" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fotothek_df_roe-neg_0002806_004_Besucher_der_Messe_in_der_Thomaskirche_zu_Ehren_Bachs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>28 July 1950: memorial service for Bach in Leipzig's Thomaskirche, on the 200th anniversary of the composer's death</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bosehaus_Leipzig_Stra%C3%9Fenfront_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Bosehaus_Leipzig_Stra%C3%9Fenfront_1.jpg/240px-Bosehaus_Leipzig_Stra%C3%9Fenfront_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="376" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Bosehaus_Leipzig_Stra%C3%9Fenfront_1.jpg/360px-Bosehaus_Leipzig_Stra%C3%9Fenfront_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Bosehaus_Leipzig_Stra%C3%9Fenfront_1.jpg/480px-Bosehaus_Leipzig_Stra%C3%9Fenfront_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2038" data-file-height="3193" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bosehaus_Leipzig_Stra%C3%9Fenfront_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bosehaus in Leipzig where the <a href="/wiki/Bach_Archive" title="Bach Archive">Bach Archive</a> has been housed since 1985</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grave_of_Johann_Sebastian_Bach_and_altar_Leipzig.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Grave_of_Johann_Sebastian_Bach_and_altar_Leipzig.JPG/240px-Grave_of_Johann_Sebastian_Bach_and_altar_Leipzig.JPG" decoding="async" width="240" height="360" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Grave_of_Johann_Sebastian_Bach_and_altar_Leipzig.JPG/360px-Grave_of_Johann_Sebastian_Bach_and_altar_Leipzig.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Grave_of_Johann_Sebastian_Bach_and_altar_Leipzig.JPG/480px-Grave_of_Johann_Sebastian_Bach_and_altar_Leipzig.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3168" data-file-height="4752" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grave_of_Johann_Sebastian_Bach_and_altar_Leipzig.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bach's grave and altar in the <a href="/wiki/St._Thomas_Church,_Leipzig" title="St. Thomas Church, Leipzig">St.&#160;Thomas Church, Leipzig</a></div></div></div>
<p>Throughout the 18th century, the appreciation of Bach's music was mostly limited to distinguished connoisseurs. The 19th century started with publication of the first biography of the composer and ended with the completion of the publication of all of Bach's known works by the <a href="/wiki/Bach_Gesellschaft" title="Bach Gesellschaft">Bach Gesellschaft</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Bach_Revival" class="mw-redirect" title="Bach Revival">Bach Revival</a> had started from <a href="/wiki/Felix_Mendelssohn" title="Felix Mendelssohn">Mendelssohn</a>'s performance of the <i><a href="/wiki/St_Matthew_Passion" title="St Matthew Passion">St Matthew Passion</a></i> in 1829. Soon after that performance, Bach started to become regarded as one of the greatest composers of all times, if not the greatest, a reputation he has retained ever since. A new extensive Bach biography was published in the second half of the 19th century.
</p><p>In the 20th century, Bach's music was widely performed and recorded, while the <a href="/wiki/Neue_Bachgesellschaft" title="Neue Bachgesellschaft">Neue Bachgesellschaft</a>, among others, published research on the composer. Modern adaptations of Bach's music contributed greatly to his popularisation in the second half of the 20th century. Among these were the <a href="/wiki/The_Swingle_Singers" title="The Swingle Singers">Swingle Singers</a>' versions of Bach pieces (for instance, the <i lang="fr" title="French language text">Air</i> from <a href="/wiki/Orchestral_suites_(Bach)#Suite_No._3_in_D_major,_BWV_1068" title="Orchestral suites (Bach)">Orchestral Suite No.&#160;3</a>, or the <i lang="de" title="German language text">Wachet auf...</i> chorale prelude) and <a href="/wiki/Wendy_Carlos" title="Wendy Carlos">Wendy Carlos</a>' 1968 <i><a href="/wiki/Switched-On_Bach" title="Switched-On Bach">Switched-On Bach</a></i>, which used the <a href="/wiki/Moog_synthesizer" title="Moog synthesizer">Moog electronic synthesiser</a>.
</p><p>By the end of the 20th century, more classical performers were gradually moving away from the performance style and instrumentation that were established in the romantic era: they started to perform Bach's music on <a href="/wiki/List_of_period_instruments#Baroque_(1600–1750)" title="List of period instruments">period instruments of the baroque era</a>, studied and practised playing techniques and tempi as established in his time, and reduced the size of instrumental ensembles and choirs to what he would have employed. The <a href="/wiki/BACH_motif" title="BACH motif">BACH motif</a>, used by the composer in his own compositions, was used in dozens of tributes to the composer from the 19th century to the 21st. In the 21st century, the complete extant output of the composer became available on-line, with several websites exclusively dedicated to him.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="18th_century">18th century</span></h3>
<p>In his own time, Bach's reputation equalled that of Telemann, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Heinrich_Graun" title="Carl Heinrich Graun">Graun</a> and Handel.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> During his life, Bach received public recognition, such as the title of court composer by <a href="/wiki/Augustus_III_of_Poland" title="Augustus III of Poland">Augustus III of Poland</a> and the appreciation he was shown by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_the_Great" title="Frederick the Great">Frederick the Great</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Karl_von_Keyserling" title="Hermann Karl von Keyserling">Hermann Karl von Keyserling</a>. Such highly placed appreciation contrasted with the humiliations he had to cope with, for instance in his hometown of Leipzig.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Also in the contemporary press, Bach had his detractors, such as <a href="/wiki/Johann_Adolf_Scheibe" title="Johann Adolf Scheibe">Johann Adolf Scheibe</a>, suggesting he write less complex music, and his supporters, such as <a href="/wiki/Johann_Mattheson" title="Johann Mattheson">Johann Mattheson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lorenz_Christoph_Mizler" title="Lorenz Christoph Mizler">Lorenz Christoph Mizler</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After his death, Bach's reputation as a composer at first declined: his work was regarded as old-fashioned compared to the emerging <a href="/wiki/Galant_music" title="Galant music">galant</a> style.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Initially, he was remembered more as a virtuoso player of the organ and as a teacher. The bulk of the music that had been <a href="/wiki/List_of_compositions_by_Johann_Sebastian_Bach_printed_during_his_lifetime" title="List of compositions by Johann Sebastian Bach printed during his lifetime">printed during the composer's lifetime</a>, at least the part that was remembered, was for the organ and the harpsichord. Thus, his reputation as a composer was initially mostly limited to his keyboard music, and that even fairly limited to its value in music education.
</p><p>Bach's surviving family members, who inherited a large part of his manuscripts, were not all equally concerned with preserving them, leading to considerable losses.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Philipp_Emanuel_Bach" title="Carl Philipp Emanuel Bach">Carl Philipp Emanuel</a>, his second eldest son, was most active in safeguarding his father's legacy: he co-authored his father's obituary, contributed to the publication of his four-part chorales,<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> staged some of his works, and the bulk of previously unpublished works of his father were preserved with his help.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Friedemann_Bach" title="Wilhelm Friedemann Bach">Wilhelm Friedemann</a>, the eldest son, performed several of his father's cantatas in <a href="/wiki/Halle_(Saale)" title="Halle (Saale)">Halle</a> but after becoming unemployed sold part of the large collection of his father's works he owned.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Several <a href="/wiki/List_of_students_of_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" class="mw-redirect" title="List of students of Johann Sebastian Bach">students of the old master</a>, such as his son-in-law <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christoph_Altnickol" title="Johann Christoph Altnickol">Johann Christoph Altnickol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Agricola" title="Johann Friedrich Agricola">Johann Friedrich Agricola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Kirnberger" title="Johann Kirnberger">Johann Kirnberger</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Ludwig_Krebs" title="Johann Ludwig Krebs">Johann Ludwig Krebs</a>, contributed to the dissemination of his legacy. The early devotees were not all musicians; for example, in Berlin, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Itzig" title="Daniel Itzig">Daniel Itzig</a>, a high official of Frederick the Great's court, venerated Bach.<sup id="cite_ref-Wolff2005_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff2005-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> His eldest daughters took lessons from Kirnberger and their sister Sara from <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Friedemann_Bach" title="Wilhelm Friedemann Bach">Wilhelm Friedemann Bach</a>, who was in Berlin from 1774 to 1784.<sup id="cite_ref-Wolff2005_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff2005-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Apple14_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apple14-183">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Sara Itzig Levy became an avid collector of works by Johann Sebastian Bach and his sons and was a "patron" of CPE Bach.<sup id="cite_ref-Apple14_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apple14-183">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While in Leipzig, performances of Bach's church music were limited to some of his motets, and under <a href="/wiki/Thomaskantor" title="Thomaskantor">cantor</a> <a href="/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Doles" title="Johann Friedrich Doles">Doles</a> some of his <a href="/wiki/Passions_(Bach)" title="Passions (Bach)">Passions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> A new generation of Bach aficionados emerged: they studiously collected and copied his music, including some of his large-scale works such as the <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_B_minor" title="Mass in B minor">Mass in B minor</a> and performed it privately. One such connoisseur was <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_van_Swieten" title="Gottfried van Swieten">Gottfried van Swieten</a>, a high-ranking Austrian official who was instrumental in passing Bach's legacy on to the composers of the <a href="/wiki/First_Viennese_School" title="First Viennese School">Viennese school</a>. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Haydn" title="Joseph Haydn">Haydn</a> owned manuscript copies of the <i>Well-Tempered Clavier</i> and the Mass in B minor and was influenced by Bach's music. <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart</a> owned a copy of one of Bach's motets,<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> transcribed some of his instrumental works (<a href="/w/index.php?title=K._404a&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="K. 404a (page does not exist)">K. 404a</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=K._405&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="K. 405 (page does not exist)">405</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> and wrote contrapuntal music influenced by his style.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-apbrown_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apbrown-189">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Beethoven</a> played the entire <i>Well-Tempered Clavier</i> by the time he was 11 and described Bach as <i lang="de" title="German language text">Urvater der Harmonie</i> (progenitor of harmony).<sup id="cite_ref-McKay_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKay-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schenk1959p101_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schenk1959p101-191">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bach_Revival" class="mw-redirect" title="Bach Revival">Bach Revival</a> and <a href="/wiki/St_Matthew_Passion#19th_century" title="St Matthew Passion">St Matthew Passion §&#160;19th century</a></div>
<p>In 1802, Johann Nikolaus Forkel published <i><a href="/wiki/Ueber_Johann_Sebastian_Bachs_Leben,_Kunst_und_Kunstwerke" class="mw-redirect" title="Ueber Johann Sebastian Bachs Leben, Kunst und Kunstwerke">Ueber Johann Sebastian Bachs Leben, Kunst und Kunstwerke</a></i>, the first biography of the composer, which contributed to his becoming known to a wider public.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> In 1805, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Mendelssohn" class="mw-redirect" title="Abraham Mendelssohn">Abraham Mendelssohn</a>, who had married one of Itzig's granddaughters, bought a substantial collection of Bach manuscripts that had come down from C.&#160;P.&#160;E.&#160;Bach, and donated it to the <a href="/wiki/Sing-Akademie_zu_Berlin" title="Sing-Akademie zu Berlin">Berlin Sing-Akademie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wolff2005_182-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff2005-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> The Sing-Akademie occasionally performed Bach's works in public concerts, for instance his <a href="/wiki/BWV_1052" class="mw-redirect" title="BWV 1052">first keyboard concerto</a>, with Sara Itzig Levy at the piano.<sup id="cite_ref-Wolff2005_182-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolff2005-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first decades of the 19th century saw an increasing number of first publications of Bach's music: Breitkopf started publishing chorale preludes,<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Hoffmeister harpsichord music,<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> and the <i>Well-Tempered Clavier</i> was printed concurrently by Simrock (Germany), Nägeli (Switzerland) and Hoffmeister (Germany and Austria) in 1801.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Vocal music was also published: motets in 1802 and 1803, followed by the E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> major version of the <i><a href="/wiki/Magnificat_(Bach)" title="Magnificat (Bach)">Magnificat</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/BWV_234" class="mw-redirect" title="BWV 234">Kyrie-Gloria Mass in A major</a>, and the cantata <a href="/wiki/Ein_feste_Burg_ist_unser_Gott,_BWV_80" title="Ein feste Burg ist unser Gott, BWV 80"><i>Ein feste Burg ist unser Gott</i> (BWV 80)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> In 1818, <a href="/wiki/Hans_Georg_N%C3%A4geli" title="Hans Georg Nägeli">Hans Georg Nägeli</a> called the <i>Mass in B minor</i> the greatest composition ever.<sup id="cite_ref-McKay_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKay-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Bach's influence was felt in the next generation of early Romantic composers.<sup id="cite_ref-Schenk1959p101_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schenk1959p101-191">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> When Felix Mendelssohn, Abraham's son, aged 13, produced his first <i>Magnificat</i> setting in 1822, it is clear that he was inspired by the then unpublished D major version of Bach's <i>Magnificat</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Felix Mendelssohn significantly contributed to the renewed interest in Bach's work with his 1829 Berlin performance of the <i><a href="/wiki/St_Matthew_Passion" title="St Matthew Passion">St&#160;Matthew Passion</a></i>, which was instrumental in setting off what has been called the Bach Revival. The <i><a href="/wiki/St_John_Passion" title="St John Passion">St John Passion</a></i> saw its 19th-century premiere in 1833, and the first performance of the <i>Mass in B minor</i> followed in 1844. Besides these and other public performances and an increased coverage on the composer and his compositions in printed media, the 1830s and 1840s also saw the first publication of more vocal works by Bach: six cantatas, the <i>St Matthew Passion</i>, and the <i>Mass in B minor</i>. A series of organ compositions saw their first publication in 1833.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Chopin" title="Frédéric Chopin">Chopin</a> started composing his <a href="/wiki/24_Preludes,_Op._28_(Chopin)" class="mw-redirect" title="24 Preludes, Op. 28 (Chopin)">24 Preludes, Op. 28</a>, inspired by the <i>Well-Tempered Clavier</i>, in 1835, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Schumann" title="Robert Schumann">Schumann</a> published his <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sechs_Fugen_%C3%BCber_den_Namen_B-A-C-H&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sechs Fugen über den Namen B-A-C-H (page does not exist)">Sechs Fugen über den Namen B-A-C-H</a></i> in 1845. Bach's music was transcribed and arranged to suit contemporary tastes and performance practice by composers such as <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Zelter" title="Carl Friedrich Zelter">Carl Friedrich Zelter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Franz" title="Robert Franz">Robert Franz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Franz_Liszt" title="Franz Liszt">Franz Liszt</a>, or combined with new music such as the melody line of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Gounod" title="Charles Gounod">Charles Gounod</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ave_Maria_(Bach/Gounod)" title="Ave Maria (Bach/Gounod)">Ave Maria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-McKay_190-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKay-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Brahms" title="Johannes Brahms">Brahms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anton_Bruckner" title="Anton Bruckner">Bruckner</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Wagner</a> were among the composers who promoted Bach's music or wrote glowingly about it.
</p><p>In 1850, the <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Bach_Gesellschaft" title="Bach Gesellschaft">Bach-Gesellschaft</a></i> (Bach Society) was founded to promote Bach's music. In the second half of the 19th century, the Society published a comprehensive edition of the composer's works. Also in the second half of the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Philipp_Spitta" title="Philipp Spitta">Philipp Spitta</a> published <i><a href="/wiki/Spitta%27s_Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Spitta&#39;s Johann Sebastian Bach">Johann Sebastian Bach</a></i>, the standard work on Bach's life and music.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> By that time, Bach was known as the first of the <a href="/wiki/Three_Bs" title="Three Bs">three Bs</a> in music. Throughout the 19th century, 200 books were published on Bach. By the end of the century, local Bach societies were established in several cities, and his music had been performed in all major musical centres.<sup id="cite_ref-McKay_190-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKay-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Germany all throughout the century, Bach was coupled to nationalist feelings, and the composer was inscribed in a religious revival. In England, Bach was coupled to an existing revival of religious and baroque music. By the end of the century, Bach was firmly established as one of the greatest composers, recognised for both his instrumental and his vocal music.<sup id="cite_ref-McKay_190-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKay-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span></h3>
<p>During the 20th century, the process of recognising the musical as well as the pedagogic value of some of the works continued, as in the promotion of the <a href="/wiki/Cello_Suites_(Bach)" title="Cello Suites (Bach)">cello suites</a> by <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Casals" title="Pablo Casals">Pablo Casals</a>, the first major performer to record these suites.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Leading performers of classical music, such as <a href="/wiki/Herbert_von_Karajan" title="Herbert von Karajan">Herbert von Karajan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Grumiaux" title="Arthur Grumiaux">Arthur Grumiaux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helmut_Walcha" title="Helmut Walcha">Helmut Walcha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wanda_Landowska" title="Wanda Landowska">Wanda Landowska</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Richter_(conductor)" title="Karl Richter (conductor)">Karl Richter</a>, <a href="/wiki/I_Musici" title="I Musici">I Musici</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_Fischer-Dieskau" title="Dietrich Fischer-Dieskau">Dietrich Fischer-Dieskau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Gould" title="Glenn Gould">Glenn Gould</a>, recorded his music.
</p><p>A significant development in the later part of the 20th century was the momentum gained by the <a href="/wiki/Historically_informed_performance" title="Historically informed performance">historically informed performance</a> practice, with forerunners such as <a href="/wiki/Nikolaus_Harnoncourt" title="Nikolaus Harnoncourt">Nikolaus Harnoncourt</a> acquiring prominence by their performances of Bach's music. His keyboard music was again performed more on the instruments Bach was familiar than on modern pianos and 19th-century romantic organs. Ensembles playing and singing Bach's music not only kept to the instruments and the performance style of his day but were also reduced to the size of the groups Bach used for his performances.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> But that was far from the only way Bach's music came to the forefront in the 20th century: his music was heard in versions ranging from <a href="/wiki/Ferruccio_Busoni" title="Ferruccio Busoni">Ferruccio Busoni</a>'s late romantic <a href="/wiki/Bach-Busoni_Editions" title="Bach-Busoni Editions">piano transcriptions</a> to jazzy interpretations such as those by <a href="/wiki/The_Swingle_Singers" title="The Swingle Singers">The Swingle Singers</a>, orchestrations like the one opening <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Fantasia_(1940_film)" title="Fantasia (1940 film)">Fantasia</a></i> movie, and synthesiser performances such as <a href="/wiki/Wendy_Carlos" title="Wendy Carlos">Wendy Carlos</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Switched-On_Bach" title="Switched-On Bach">Switched-On Bach</a></i> recordings.
</p><p>Bach's music has influenced other genres. For instance, jazz musicians have adopted Bach's music, with <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Loussier" title="Jacques Loussier">Jacques Loussier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ian_Anderson" title="Ian Anderson">Ian Anderson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uri_Caine" title="Uri Caine">Uri Caine</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Modern_Jazz_Quartet" title="Modern Jazz Quartet">Modern Jazz Quartet</a> among those creating jazz versions of his works.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Several 20th-century composers referred to Bach or his music, for example <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Ysa%C3%BFe" title="Eugène Ysaÿe">Eugène Ysaÿe</a> in <a href="/wiki/Six_Sonatas_for_solo_violin_(Ysa%C3%BFe)" title="Six Sonatas for solo violin (Ysaÿe)">Six Sonatas for solo violin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Shostakovich" title="Dmitri Shostakovich">Dmitri Shostakovich</a> in <a href="/wiki/24_Preludes_and_Fugues_(Shostakovich)" title="24 Preludes and Fugues (Shostakovich)">24 Preludes and Fugues</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heitor_Villa-Lobos" title="Heitor Villa-Lobos">Heitor Villa-Lobos</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Bachianas_Brasileiras" title="Bachianas Brasileiras">Bachianas Brasileiras</a></i>. All kinds of publications involved Bach: not only were there the <i>Bach Jahrbuch</i> publications of the Neue Bachgesellschaft, various other studies and biographies by among others <a href="/wiki/Albert_Schweitzer" title="Albert Schweitzer">Albert Schweitzer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sanford_Terry_(historian)" title="Charles Sanford Terry (historian)">Charles Sanford Terry</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Butt_(musician)" title="John Butt (musician)">John Butt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Wolff" title="Christoph Wolff">Christoph Wolff</a>, and the 1950 first edition of the <a href="/wiki/Bach-Werke-Verzeichnis" title="Bach-Werke-Verzeichnis">Bach-Werke-Verzeichnis</a>, but also books such as <i><a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del,_Escher,_Bach" title="Gödel, Escher, Bach">Gödel, Escher, Bach</a></i> put the composer's art in a wider perspective. Bach's music was extensively listened to, performed, broadcast, arranged, adapted, and commented upon in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-KVNM2000_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KVNM2000-207">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Around 2000, the 250th anniversary of Bach's death, three record companies issued box sets with complete recordings of Bach's music.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bach's music features three times—more than that of any other composer—on the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_Golden_Record" title="Voyager Golden Record">Voyager Golden Record</a>, a gramophone record containing a broad sample of the images, common sounds, languages, and music of Earth, sent into outer space with the two <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a> probes.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Tributes to Bach in the 20th century include statues erected in his honour and a variety of things such as streets and space objects being named after him.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> Also, a multitude of musical ensembles such as the <a href="/wiki/Bach_Aria_Group" title="Bach Aria Group">Bach Aria Group</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bachsolisten" title="Deutsche Bachsolisten">Deutsche Bachsolisten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bachchor_Stuttgart" title="Bachchor Stuttgart">Bachchor Stuttgart</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bach_Collegium_Japan" title="Bach Collegium Japan">Bach Collegium Japan</a> adopted the composer's name. <a href="/wiki/Bach_festival" title="Bach festival">Bach festivals</a> were held on several continents, and competitions and prizes such as the <a href="/wiki/International_Johann_Sebastian_Bach_Competition" title="International Johann Sebastian Bach Competition">International Johann Sebastian Bach Competition</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy_of_Music_Bach_Prize" title="Royal Academy of Music Bach Prize">Royal Academy of Music Bach Prize</a> were named after the composer. While by the end of the 19th century Bach had been inscribed in nationalism and religious revival, the late 20th century saw Bach as the subject of a secularised art-as-religion (<i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Kunstreligion" title="Kunstreligion">Kunstreligion</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-McKay_190-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKay-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-KVNM2000_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KVNM2000-207">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span></h3>
<p>In the 21st century, Bach's compositions have become available online, for instance at the <a href="/wiki/International_Music_Score_Library_Project" title="International Music Score Library Project">International Music Score Library Project</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> High-resolution facsimiles of Bach's autographs became available at the Bach digital website.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>21st-century biographers include <a href="/wiki/Peter_Williams_(Musicologist)" class="mw-redirect" title="Peter Williams (Musicologist)">Peter Williams</a> and the conductor <a href="/wiki/John_Eliot_Gardiner" title="John Eliot Gardiner">John Eliot Gardiner</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burial_site">Burial site</span></h3>
<p>Bach was originally buried at Old St. John's Cemetery in Leipzig. His grave went unmarked for nearly 150 years, but in 1894 his remains were located and moved to a vault in St. John's Church. This building was destroyed by <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Leipzig_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Leipzig in World War II">Allied bombing during World War&#160;II</a>, so in 1950 Bach's remains were taken to their present grave in St. Thomas Church.<sup id="cite_ref-Baroquenet_20-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baroquenet-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Later research has called into question whether the remains in the grave are actually those of Bach.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognition_in_Protestant_churches">Recognition in Protestant churches</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Episcopal_Church)" title="Calendar of saints (Episcopal Church)">liturgical calendar of the Episcopal Church</a> remembers Bach annually with a <a href="/wiki/Feast_day" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast day">feast day</a> on 28 July, together with George Frideric Handel and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Purcell" title="Henry Purcell">Henry Purcell</a>; on the same day, the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Lutheran)" title="Calendar of saints (Lutheran)">Calendar of Saints</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran Church">Lutheran Church</a> remembers Bach and Handel with <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Sch%C3%BCtz" title="Heinrich Schütz">Heinrich Schütz</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
